==PAGE:3==
Abstract
In recent years, concrete structures containing sulfide-bearing aggregates have caused problems in several
countries. The iron sulfide mineral pyrrhotite (Fe1−xS) has been identified as the main reason for the
failure, as it is highly reactive in the presence of water and oxygen. It will oxidize and release sulfur,
which can cause a sulfate attack in the concrete.
In this study, three test methods are used to investigate the oxidation of a sulfide-bearing aggregate
with high contents of pyrrhotite. Seven different solutions are tested to investigate the optimal solution
to elevate the oxidation of pyrrhotite: 0.4 M NaOCl, 1 M NaOCl, 1 M NaCl, 0.1 M Na2SO4, 1 M
Na2CO3, 1 M H2O2, and 5 M H2O2. The duration of the three testing methods varies between 90
minutes, 3 hours, and 12 weeks. The two fastest methods test only the aggregate: a leaching test with
5 g of aggregate and 500 mL tested at both 40 °C and 60 °C, and an oxygen measurement test where
a mix of aggregate and solution is placed in an airtight cylinder with a connected oxygen meter that
measures the oxygen concentration within the cylinder. The last test method investigates the oxidation
of pyrrhotite, where the aggregate is added as filler in a cement paste with a w/b-ratio of 65 % and
with the aggregate as filler with a 35% replacement of the cement. Samples of Ø50 mm and H10 mm
are placed in a unidirectional semi-immersed test setup, where only the bottom of the samples are in
contact with the solutions and the transport of the solution in the cement paste happens due to capillary
action and diffusion.
Depending on the different test methods, different solutions are proven the most successful. In the
leaching test, 5 M H2O2 accelerated the oxidation and sulfur release of the aggregate at 60 °C. However,
the solution is unsuccessful in the other two tests.
In the oxygen measurement test, a solution of 1 M NaCl increased the oxygen consumption of 14.4
% of the aggregate within the test duration of 3 hours, which is higher than when the aggregate is
mixed with deionized water (3-5 % oxygen consumption). The increased oxidation using a solution of
NaCl can be due to mechanisms similar to the pitting corrosion of steel.
In the cement paste test, solutions of NaOCl are proven most successful in elevating the oxidation
of pyrrhotite in a cementitious environment. In the SEM analysis, evidence of oxidized pyrrhotite is
found. However, from the XRD analysis, there is no evidence of an occurring chemical sulfate attack
in the samples in the form of the formation of ettringite. Depending on whether only the aggregate
is tested or if the aggregate is used in a cement paste or concrete, different solutions are appropriate.
While a solution of H2O2 might cause a release of sulfur, the solution is not applicable in a cementitious
environment, because of its low pH, which can cause a destabilization of the hydration products. Here
a solution with high pH, like NaOCl, is preferred to keep the hydration products stable during the test.
==PAGE:4==
ii
==PAGE:5==
Resumé
I løbet af de seneste år har betonkonstruktioner indeholdende sulfidholdige tilslag forårsaget problemer
i flere lande. Det er vurderet at pyrrhotit (Fe1−xS) er hovedårsagen til disse problemer, da det er meget
reaktivt i kontakt med ilt og vand og kan oxidere, hvilket frigiver svovl, som kan lede til et sulfatangreb
i betonen. I denne rapport anvendes der tre forskellige testmetoder til at undersøge tilslag med et højt
indhold a pyrrhotit.
Syv forskellige opløsninger undersøges for at bestemme den optimale opløsning til at fremskynde
oxideringen af pyrrhotit: 0.4 M NaOCl, 1 M NaOCl, 1 M NaCl, 0.1 M Na2SO4, 1 M Na2CO3, 1 M
H2O2 og 5 M H2O2. Længden af de tre testmetoder varierer mellem 90 minutter, 3 timer og 12 uger.
De to hurtigste metoder tester kun tilslaget. (1) måler hvor meget svovl og jern som frigives hvis man
tilsætter 5 g til 500 mL af hver af syv opløsninger ved både 40 °C og 60 °C. (2) måler koncentrationen
af ilt i en lufttæt cylinder, hvori der er placeret tilslag blandet sammen med en af de syv opløsninger.
(3) undersøger oxideringen af pyrrhotit, hvor tilslaget er tilsat som filler i en cementpasta med et v/b-
forhold på 65 % og hvor 35% af cementen er erstattet med et sulfidholdigt tilslag. Prøver på Ø50
mm og H10 mm er placeret i en forsøgsopstilling således, at kun bunden af prøverne er i kontakt med
opløsningerne og transporten af opløsningen i cementpastaen skyldes kapillarsugning og diffusion.
Afhængigt af de forskellige testmetoder er forskellige opløsninger de mest succesfulde. I det første
forsøg accelererer 5 M H2O2 oxideringen og frigivelsen af svovl ved 60 °C, men mislykkes i de to øvrige.
I det anden forsøg øger en opløsning af 1 M NaCl iltforbruget til 14,4 % af tilslaget inden for 3 timer
sammenlignet med iltforbruget af aggregatet blandet med demineraliseret vand, som har et iltforbrug
på mellem 3-5 % oxygen. Den øgede oxidering ved hjælp af en NaCl opløsning kan skyldes mekanismer
svarende til grubetæring af stål.
I det tredje forsøg viser det sig, at opløsninger af NaOCl bedst accelererer oxideringen af pyrrhotit
i en cementpasta, da der i SEM-analysen er fundet tegn på oxideret pyrrhotit. Dog er der fra XRD-
analysen imidlertid ingen tegn er på, at der skulle være et kemisk sulfat angreb undervejs i prøverne,
da der ikke blev fundet en øget mængde ettringit.
Afhængigt af om det kun er tilslag, som testes eller om det er inkorporeret i en cementpasta, er det
forskellige opløsninger som vurderes optimale. Mens en opløsning af H2O2 kan forårsage frigivelse af
svovl, er opløsningen ikke anvendelig i en cementpasta på grund af dens lave pH, hvilket kan føre til
en destabilisering af de cementholdige produkter. Her foretrækkes en opløsning med høj pH, ligesom
NaOCl, for at holde de hydratiseringsprodukter stabile under testen.

Preface
The oxidation and sulfur release from sulfide-bearing aggergates was investigated as a master project
in Civil Engineering at the Technical University of Denmark (DTU) by MSc. student Sigrid Glargaard
in the period 01.09.2023-01.03.2024 in partial fulfillment for the degree Master of Science in Civil
Engineering. The scope of the work corresponds to a total of 35 ECTS.
The project was done with the supervisors Wolfgang Kunther, associate professor at the Department of
Environmental and Resource Engineering, and Diego Jesus De Souza, Post-doc also at the Department
of Environmental and Resource Engineering.
A great thanks to the supervisors and the employees in the laboratories of DTU for their assistance and
guidance. Also thanks to friends and family for the proofreading of this project.

==PAGE:13==
CHAPTER 1
Introduction
1.1
Motivation
After water, concrete is the most used material in the world [1]. The common constituents in concrete
are water, cement, sand, and gravel, also known as aggregates, but the demand for sand and gravel for
construction is rising faster than the natural sources can sustain. As the demand for sand and gravel
increases in construction, so does the need for aggregates that are ”safe to use” in construction [1].
Not all types of aggregate are safe to use in concrete, and in some countries, there are cases of
concrete structures that have sustained significant damage due to the presence of ”unsafe” aggregates.
Some aggregates that are particularly known to cause issues, such as extensive cracking, loss of material
integrity, or even complete failure in the concrete structure due to sulfate attack, are aggregates con-
taining sulfides. It is therefore specified in the standards to limit the sulfur content in the aggregates to
prevent a sulfate attack in the concrete structures. However, sulfides are present in most rock lithologies,
and at times it can be the most convenient and economical solution to use aggregate readily available
at the site as the import of ”safe” aggregates can be diﬀicult and expensive. [2, 3].
In recent years, concrete structures containing sulfide-bearing aggregates have caused problems in
the Trois-Rivières area (Canada), where house foundations have shown deterioration 3-5 years after
construction. The iron sulfide mineral pyrrhotite (Fe1−xS) has been identified as the main reason for
the failure, as it is highly reactive in the presence of water and oxygen and will oxidize and release
sulfur. Also in housing in Connecticut (USA), and Donegal (Ireland), pyrrhotite has been linked to the
deterioration of concrete structures [2, 4, 5].
Therefore, it is also stated in the European standard, EN 12620, that if pyrrhotite is detected in
the aggregate, the sulfur content must be limited to 0.1 % by mass [6]. However, it has been proven
diﬀicult to characterize the aggregate accurately due to the uneven distribution of pyrrhotite within the
material. The oxidation of the mineral is a complex process that is influenced by several factors. How
to characterize the presence of pyrrhotite is not mentioned in the European standard and there is no
standard laboratory test exists to assess the potential deleterious character of sulfide-bearing aggregates
[7].
The degradation of cementitious materials due to sulfate ions is commonly divided into a physical
and chemical sulfate attack. While the physical attacks are dominant in many situations in the field,
the chemical attack is the one that is primarily studied in the laboratory [8]. As the release of sulfur
within the concrete from sulfide-bearing aggregates increases the risk of a sulfate attack, there is a need
to assess the mechanisms of a sulfate attack that follows the release of sulfur from the aggregates.
==PAGE:14==
2
1 Introduction
1.2
Project statement
Based on the issues listed in Section 1.1 regarding sulfide-bearing aggregates and the risk of sulfate
attack in concrete structures, the objective of the thesis is to address the following questions:
• What exposure conditions result in elevated oxidation and sulfur release of sulfide-bearing aggre-
gates?
• Is it possible to create a test setup where the oxidation rate in sulfide-bearing aggregates can be
determined?
• How does the oxidation and release of sulfur affect the cementitious materials and is it possible
to initiate a sulfate attack from the sulfur release?
The research questions are addressed through three test methods, ranging from a duration of 90 minutes
to 3 hours of testing only the aggregate and 12 weeks of testing the sulfide-bearing aggregate in a cement
paste. The test methods are listed below.
• Leaching test of an iron sulfide-bearing aggregate in the different exposure.
• Oxidation consumption of the aggregate by measurement of the oxygen concentration.
• A unidirectional semi-immersion test set up to investigate the degradation mechanisms of a sulfate
attack initiated by the sulfur release from iron sulfide-bearing aggregates.
The aggregate investigated in this thesis originates from Finland and has high levels of pyrrhotite that
exceed the allowed limit by the European standard, EN 12620 [6]. The three methods are analyzed and
compared to give an understanding of the oxidation and sulfur release of the sulfide-bearing aggregate.
Seven different solutions are used to investigate their effect on the aggregate alone and as a filler
material in a cement paste.
The solutions used are: NaOCl, NaCl, Na2SO4, Na2CO3, and H2O2.
The different test setups allow us to investigate the oxidation of sulfide-bearing aggregates in several
exposure conditions to determine in what conditions the oxidation and sulfate release are elevated and
detrimental to concrete structures.
==PAGE:15==
CHAPTER 2
Theoretical background
2.1
Portland cement
Portland cement is a hydraulic binder, that is composed of clinker minerals and often gypsum, which
hardens in the presence of water. The clinker minerals are produced by burning the raw materials (clay
and limestone minerals) in a cement kiln at temperatures of about 1450 °C, and then mixed with a few
percent of gypsum, and finely ground into cement [9]. The cement clinker consists mainly of the oxides
SiO2, CaO, Al2O2, and Fe2O3. By burning the clinker they form the bogue compounds: Alite (C3S),
Belite (C2S), Aluminate (C3A) and Ferrite (C4AF) [9, 10].
2.1.1
Hydration of Portland cement
The four bogue compounds will react and form the hydration products: portlandite, C-S-H gel, mono-
sulfate, and ettringite. The C-S-H gel binds the cement paste and is the most significant strength-giving
component in the hardened cement paste. Monosulfate can bind chloride ions and in Friedel’s salt. In
the reactions described next, a cement chemical notation is used for simplicity. Name, chemical compo-
sition, and cement chemical notation of some relevant chemicals, the bogue compounds, and the phases
formed in the hydration process are presented in Table 2.1.
Table 2.1: Relevant cement chemical notations [11].

C3A is important for the early strength of the cement, but the hydration of C3A is fast and can cause
a quick setting of the cement that will reduce the workability. However, adding gypsum to the cement
delays the reaction by forming ettringite. Ettringite will then react with C3A and form monosulfate.
The hydration process is hereby delayed.

In contrast to the other clinker minerals, C4AF does not contribute to the strength development of the
cement paste [10].
2.2
Oxidation of sulfide minerals
The iron sulfides pyrite (Fe2S) and pyrrhotite (Fe1−xS) are two common and widespread sulfide minerals
that can be found in rocks. These sulfide minerals are unstable in the presence of oxygen and water
and tend to oxidize. However, pyrrhotite is known to be more reactive than pyrite. Pyrrhotite has
a non-stoichiometric composition Fe1−xS, where x varies between 0-0.125. The uneven distribution of
sulfide minerals in the aggregate material makes it very diﬀicult to classify the mineral content even in
the quarries, where some areas might contain more pyrrhotite than others [12].
Pyrrhotite can be dissolved rapidly under acidic conditions or in a slower process by oxidation and
form Fe2+ and H2S. The oxidation of iron sulfides in the presence of water and oxygen forms acidic,
iron and sulfate-rich by-products.
The oxidation of pyrrhotite with oxygen and water is shown in
Equation (2.8) and the reaction with acid is shown in Equation (2.9) [7, 13]
Fe1−xS

The oxidation of Fe2+ forms Fe3+ ions, that can precipitate out of the solution and form Fe(OH)3. The

The ferric iron, Fe3+, can oxidize more pyrrhotite and increase the acidity in the system according to
Equation (2.12). If Equation (2.10) occurs in acidic condition, some Fe3+ will remain in the solution
and maintain a cyclic reaction between Equation (2.10) and Equation (2.12).
Fe1−xS
+
(8 −2x)Fe3+

(9 −3x)Fe2+
+

4
+
8H+
(2.12)
The oxidation of pyrrhotite can lead to an internal sulfate attack, where gypsum and ettringite are
formed. The release of sulfate ions from the oxidation of pyrrhotite reacts with the solid compounds in
the cement and forms gypsum [4].
2.2.1
The effect of particle size, temperature & relative humidity on oxidation
The rate at which the oxidation of pyrrhotite occurs is affected by several factors. The amount of
dissolved oxygen in the solutions, ferric iron (see Equation (2.12)), particle size of the aggregate, relative
humidity, and temperature [2, 5, 14]. Not all these factors will be investigated in this study, so the main
focus of this study will be on the effect of relative humidity and temperature. Equation (2.8) shows that
the presence of water is required for oxidation by air. The oxidation rate of sulfide minerals increases
with an increase in relative humidity and temperature [13, 14]. The range of relative humidity was
previously studied by Steger et. al between 37 % and 75 % showing a direct increase in the oxidation
rate with an increase in relative humidity [14]. The primary oxidant is oxygen, so deoxygenated water
will not oxidize sulfide minerals [13, 15].
Furthermore, the particle size affects the rate of oxidation. A smaller particle size means a larger
exposed surface area to oxidation and an increased oxidation rate, and hence a more reactive aggregate
[2]. Therefore, it has opted to use aggregates in this study with a particle size of 125 µm.
2.3
Sulfate attack
Sulfate attack is a deterioration mechanism of concrete, of which the reason behind is still discussed
[11]. However, it is commonly divided into a chemical and a physical phenomenon. The physical attack
is related to the salt crystallization usually close to the surface causing spalling on the surface due to
the alternate cycles between sulfate salts hydrated and un-hydrated form and accumulation of ions due
to drying on the surface, creating a drying front. The phenomenon has been studied using Na2SO4 and
the phase change between mirabilite and thernadite causing spalling on concrete surfaces [8, 16]. The
chemical attack is the result of a chain of chemical reactions between the hydrated Portland cement
and sulfate ions from an outside or inside source forming gypsum and ettringite. It can lead to the
degradation of the concrete by causing expansion and cracking, poor bonding between cement paste
and aggregate, decomposition of the cement paste, and spalling of the surface [10]. High humidity is
required for a sulfate attack to occur and the formation of ettringite takes place at temperatures equal
to or higher than room temperature, but it will decompose at temperatures above 65 °C [10, 11].
Sulfate attacks occur when water, monosulfate, and gypsum react and form ettringite. The volume
of ettringite is smaller than the volume of the three reactants but is larger than the volume of the solids
(monosulfate and gypsum), which will cause an expansion in the concrete. The formation of gypsum
and ettringite are shown in Equation (2.13) and Equation (2.14) respectively.
CH
+
¯S
+
H
→
C¯SH2
(2.13)
==PAGE:18==
6
2 Theoretical background
monosulfate
z
}|
{
3
 C3A · C¯S · H12

+
2
gypsum
z }| {
C¯SH2
+
16H
→
ettringite
z
}|
{
C3A · 3C¯S · H32
(2.14)
The formation of ettringite is dependent on the amount of C3A. When the formation of ettringite
happens during the hydration process of the concrete, it is considered an early ettringite formation
that does not cause any harm to the cement paste. However, if the ettringite formation is formed later,
the possible expansion of the hardened concrete is limited and the ettringite formation can produce
cracking or spalling. There are two types of ettringite-related damage: external and internal sulfate
attack. External sulfate attack is due to external sources such as sewage, industrial wastewater, and
some types of soil and can sometimes be due to sulfates in groundwater or seawater. Internal sulfate
attack happens due to already present sulfate ions in the concrete. It can be contaminated aggregates
or cement, where the gypsum content is not well-balanced [17].
2.3.1
Sulfate reactions during hydration
In cement production, gypsum is added to retard the binding of the concrete as explained in Section 2.1.
However, this early formation of ettringite does not lead to a sulfate attack. The formation of ettringite
and monosulfate are shown in Equation (2.4) and Equation (2.5).
2.3.2
External sulfate attack
External sulfate attack is caused by an external source of sulfates, and the chemical interactions between
a sulfate-rich solution or soil and the cement paste. For the external sulfate attack to occur, the concrete
must be in a sulfate-rich environment, in the presence of water, and have a high permeability for the
sulfates to penetrate the concrete [17]. If the cement is exposed to a sulfate solution containing sodium
sulfate, the formation of sodium hydroxide as a byproduct in the formation of gypsum will ensure a
highly alkaline environment, which is essential for the stability of the C-S-H gel. The formation of
gypsum from an external sulfate attack with sodium sulfate is shown in Equation (2.15) [10].
Na2SO4
+
CH
+
2H
→
C¯SH2
+
2NaOH
(2.15)
2.3.3
Internal sulfate attack
An internal sulfate attack occurs, when the source of sulfate ions is within the concrete, such as the
aggregate or cement. Furthermore, two other conditions must be applicable: high moisture and a source
of oxygen. The formation of ettringite can be delayed during the cement hydration due to too high
temperature as ettringite decomposes to form monosulfate at temperatures above 65 °C. C-S-H adsorbs
the sulfate ions from the decomposed ettringite, but they can be released later during service life and
cause a delayed ettringite formation [10].
Aggregate containing iron sulfides such as pyrite or pyrrhotite is also an internal source of sulfate.
When oxidized the mineral will release sulfate that can be a part of a delayed formation of gypsum and
ettringite as seen in Equation (2.13) and Equation (2.14).
In the following study both internal and external sulfate attacks are investigated, where the external
sulfate attack is investigated with a solution of Na2SO4 and the internal sulfate attack is investigated
with different solutions expected to elevate the oxidation of pyrrhotite. Furthermore, the degradation
mechanisms of a physical and chemical sulfate attack are studied in the cement paste samples.
==PAGE:19==
2.4 Chlorides in the cement paste
7
2.4
Chlorides in the cement paste
If chlorides penetrate the concrete, they can be harmful to the reinforcement in concrete by promoting
corrosion. Chloride ions can exist in the concrete as either free or bound chlorides. The free chloride
ions exist in the pore solution, while the bound chlorides can either be bound physically in the C-S-H
gel or chemically in Friedel’s salt. The binding of chlorides can delay the corrosion of the reinforcement,
as only the free chlorides can penetrate the concrete into the reinforcement. The transport of the free
chlorides (or other ions) can occur through the pore solution, but also through regions of increased
permeability, microcracks, and capillary action [9].
Friedel’s salt is formed when monosulfate or monocarbonate comes into contact with two free chloride
ions, where the chloride ions exchange places with the sulfate or carbonate ions. Thus, the chloride ions
are bound and can no longer do damage. Friedel’s salt is dependent on the pH in the pore solution.
Below a pH of 12, the chloride binding decreases partly due to the dissolution of the AFm phases and
approaches 0 at a pH of 9 [18]. Equation (2.16) shows the formation of Friedel’s salt from monosulfate
[19].
monosulfate
z
}|
{
Ca4Al2SO4(OH)12 · 6H2O
+
2Cl−
→
F riedel′s salt
z
}|
{
Ca4Al2Cl2(OH)12 · 4H2O
+
SO2−
4
+
2H2O (2.16)
Corrosion is an electrochemical process where some areas become negatively or positively charged,
creating respectively anodes and cathodes. Iron is dissolved in the anodes, where the iron oxide is also
deposited. In an environment where the pH is high (above 11.5), the iron is oxidized and oxides are
deposited as a thin protective film around. However, chlorides can penetrate this film, and the affected
area becomes an anode and the unaffected area becomes a cathode leading to pitting corrosion of the
metal [9].
Chlorides and sulfate are often found to coexist in concrete structures in marine and saline environ-
ments. A sulfate attack can lead to a release of the bound chlorides via the decomposition of Friedel’s
salt, where Cl−is replaced by SO2−
4
[19].
2.5
Carbonation
Carbonation is the reaction of CO2 and portlandite in the cement paste, where calcium carbonate
precipitates in the pore network, but first the CO2 must dissolve in the pore solution, which produces
CO−
3 ions that react with Ca+ ions, see Equation (2.17) [9, 20].
Ca(OH)2
+
CO2
→
CaCO3
+
H2O
(2.17)
As the dissolving of CO2 is required for the carbonation process to occur, the relative humidity plays
a role in the transport of gasses such as CO2 and the carbonation process. Atmospheric CO2 requires
water to dissolve before it can react with calcium hydroxide and form calcium carbonate. However,
if the relative humidity is too high, carbonation will significantly slow down. Therefore, the optimal
conditions for carbonation are intermediate humidities, between 50 - 70 %. The carbonation process
reduces the pH of the pore solution in the surrounding cement to a pH of about 9.
The reduced
pH within the pore solution can destabilize AFm, AFt, and the C-S-H gel. Furthermore, the low pH
destabilizes the oxide film that normally surrounds the steel in concrete, as explained in Section 2.4,
promoting corrosion of steel. As calcium carbonate occupies a greater volume than portlandite, its
formation will result in reduced porosity in the concrete. [9, 20, 21]

8

CHAPTER 3
Materials & Methodology
3.1
Origin of rocks
Three different tests are conducted to evaluate the oxidation potential of a sulfide-bearing aggregate.
The aggregates used in this project originate from the Särkiniemi quarry close to Kuopio in Finland.
The rocks come from three different parts of the quarry. The sulfur content and pyrrhotite content are
given in Table 3.1 and greatly exceed the limits given by EN 12620 [6] of 0.1 % sulfur if pyrrhotite is
detected.
Table 3.1: Values of the mineral composition of the rocks used.
Sulfur content
Pyrrhotite content
Pyrite content
[%]

Särkiniemi 3
1.7
0.65
0.39
Besides containing iron sulfides, the aggregate also consists of primarily biotite, plagioclase (other than
albite), and quartz. The exact composition of the aggregate can be found in Appendix A. The Finnish
rocks are crushed into finer parts and sieved. In this study, only aggregates of particle size <150 µm
are used in the conducted tests. The aggregate has a density of 2820 kg/m3.
3.2
Solutions
Five different solutions are chosen to evaluate the oxidation potential of the sulfide-bearing aggregate.
For consistency, every solution is investigated with a concentration of 1 M. Both NaOCl and H2O2 are
known to be strong oxidizing agents. In a test by Rodrigues et al. [4] a solution of 1 M NaOCl and
H2O2 are investigated to study the oxidation of mortar bars containing sulfide bearing aggregates in
3 hours wetting cycles. The same solutions are investigated in this study along with 0.4 M of NaOCl
and 5 M of H2O2, and 1 M concentrations of Na2SO4, Na2CO3 and NaCl. A solution of 5 M H2O2 has
previously been used in a study by Marcelino et al. [2] to test only the aggregate itself. Early on in the
tests, the cement paste samples exposed to the 1 M Na2SO4 solution cracked, therefore a solution of
0.1 M is added to the testing scheme. The solutions are investigated to see how the aggregate behaves
in a sulfate-rich environment. Similarly is 1 M of Na2CO3 chosen to see how the aggregate is acting in
a carbonate-rich solution. The NaCl solution is chosen to see the difference between a solution of NaCl
and one of NaOCl. The solutions and their concentrations are shown in Table 3.2.
==PAGE:22==
10
3 Materials & Methodology

H2O2-5M
The pH of the solutions is measured before and after testing, and the solutions are analyzed using
Ion chromatography (IC) and Inductively Coupled Plasma Optical Emission Spectrometry (ICP). IC
detects concentrations of anions in the solutions such as chloride, sulfate, and nitrate, while ICP detects
elements, such as sulfur, sodium, silicium, etc.
3.3
Leaching test
Sulfide minerals oxidize when water is present. Therefore, a simple test is set up to investigate the
oxidation of sulfide-bearing aggregate immersed in one of the seven solutions listed in Table 3.2.
A volumetric flask is filled with 500 mL of the chosen solution and 5 g ± 0.0005 g of aggregate is
added to the flask. The flask is then placed in an oven set to 40 °C on a magnetic stirrer. After 90
min the aggregate is filtered by vacuum filtration from the solution and dried at 105 °C whereafter it
is weighed again. A 20 mL sample of the solution is taken for IC/ICP analysis, see Section 3.2.
As an increase in temperature is known to accelerate the oxidation rate of sulfide minerals, the same
test is repeated at 60 °C as well. Instead of using a magnetic stirrer, the flask is shaken well before
being placed in the oven, since the stirrer only works in temperatures up to 40 °C.
The test conducted by Marcelino et al. tested the aggregate at 90 °C with a 5 M H2O2 solution [2].
However, during tests in this study, it is decided to not test the solutions at 90 degrees, but only at 60
°C and 40 °C.
The conclusion of the oxygen consumption test with H2O2 carried out by Marcelino et al.is that the
oxygen consumption increases with decreasing particle size [2]. Hence, this justifies the particle size of
125 µm that has been used during this test.
3.4
Oxygen measurement test
Sulfide minerals consume oxygen in the presence of H2O and O2, as stated in Equation (2.8).
By
monitoring the oxygen concentration, the oxidation potential can be determined for sulfide-bearing
aggregates. Based on the study by Rodrigues et al. [7], a test is set up. Here an airtight cylinder and
an oxygen meter are used to measure the oxygen consumed by the aggregate.
A cylinder with an inner diameter of 94 mm and height of 200 mm is used for the test with an oxygen
meter,MO-200 from apogee, which is connected at the center of the lid. The cylinder is filled with a mix
of the aggregate and solution to start the oxidation process. The cylinder is not filled to the top but
leaves a headspace (empty space at the top of the cylinder) to measure the oxygen concentration over 3
hours. A filled cylinder and the oxygen meter are shown in Figure 3.1. The cylinder used in this study
is smaller than the cylinder used by Rodrigues et al. [7], so the headspace is adjusted accordingly. The
diameter of the cylinder is half the width of the one used by Rodrigues et al., while the height is the
==PAGE:23==
3.5 Cement paste test
11
same. Rodrigues et al. found the optimal headspace to be 100 mm and the height of the aggregate to
be 100 mm in the cylinder [7]. With a smaller cylinder, a headspace of 25 mm is chosen with still 100
mm of aggregate. However, test setups with both headspaces are investigated. With a headspace of 25
mm, a layer of 75 mm of 2-4 mm Storebæltssand is placed in the bottom of the cylinder, to ensure that
the height of the aggregate in the cylinder does not exceed 100 mm.
Figure 3.1: Cylinder and oxygen meter.
To calculate the mass of aggregate and water needed to fill 100 mm of the cylinder, Equation (3.1) and
Equation (3.2) are used. Appendix D.1 contains the contains details regarding the used quantities of
aggregates and type of solutions of aggregate and solutions used.
magg = (1 −ngm) Vgmρagg
(3.1)
mw = VgmngmSgmρw
(3.2)
where ngm is the porosity of the ground material fixed at 50%, Vgm is the volume of the ground material,
ρagg = 2820kg/m3 is the density of the aggregate, Sgm is the saturation degree of the ground material,
and ρw is the density of the solution [7].
Before testing, the aggregate is dried at 105 °C and cooled to room temperature in a desiccator. The
aggregate and water are then mixed and the material is placed in the cylinder, by placing two layers
of equal mass and compacting it until the desired thickness is reached. The test is run for 3 hours
with measurements taken every 5 minutes in the first 30 minutes and every 15 minutes afterward. The
oxygen concentration is measured of the aggregate mixed with deionized water, but also with some of
the solutions. The following solutions are tested: 1 M NaOCl, 1 M Na2CO3, 1 M NaCl, 1 M H2O2,
and 0.1 M Na2SO4, see Table 3.2.
3.5
Cement paste test
Based on the study conducted by Wang et al. [8], a test setup is made to investigate the influence of
cement paste samples containing aggregates with a high concentration of pyrrhotite as filler. The idea
==PAGE:24==
12
3 Materials & Methodology
is to test how different solutions affect the cement paste samples in a unidirectional penetration setup,
where the samples are placed on top of plastic boxes with round holes being cut in the lid to ensure
that the only occurring transport is through the samples. The test is carried out in a climate chamber
where the temperature is set to 40 °C and the relative humidity to 50 %. Every 4 weeks the following
measurements and tests are performed: (i) determination of the potential expansion of the samples,
(ii) measurement of the mineral composition by using X-ray diffraction (XRD), (iii) the leaching to
the solution by using IC and ICP analysis and (iv) visual investigations of the samples. At the end of
the test, the microstructure of the samples is investigated by using both Scanning Electron Microscope
(SEM) and energy-dispersive X-ray spectroscopy (EDX). The transport through the sample is neither
fully nor partially immersed, but rather a unidirectional semi-immersed approach, where the sample is
only in contact with the solution by the bottom, and the top of the sample is exposed to a climate-
controlled chamber. The transport of the solution through the sample is then driven by capillary action
and diffusion [8].
3.5.1
Casting of samples
The mix design of the cement paste samples can be found in Table 3.3. Aggregate with a particle size
less than 125 µm from Särkiniemi is used as filler. The samples are cast with a filler content of 35 % and
a water-to-binder ratio of 65 %. The cement used for the test is Rapid® cement from Aalborg Portland,
with 95-100 % cement clinker and up to 5 % limestone filler and in strength class 52.5 N. The cement
contains less than 8 % C3A, and is therefore considered to be sulfate resistant [22, 23].
Table 3.3: Mix design of 1 m3 of cement paste.
Constituent
Description
Density
Mass
[kg/m3]
[kg]
Cement
Rapid®
3140
662
Filler
Särkiniemi quarry
2800
356
Water
-
1000
662
The samples are mixed in a blender for one minute and cast in 125 mL plastic bottles. Between the
bottle and lid, a layer of plastic wrap is added such that the air between the two is not mixed into the
paste. Directly after casting the samples are set to rotate for 24 h at 3 rpm and then put in a curing
room at 20 °C for 14 days. Note that samples exposed to 0.1 M Na2SO4 and reference samples did not
rotate for 24 h, as the machine stopped. For how long is unknown. Pictures from the casting of the
samples are shown in Figure 3.2.

the cement paste.

3.5 Cement paste test
13
3.5.2
Preparation of samples
14 days after casting the plastic is removed from the samples and they are cut into 10 mm thick samples
with the saw Discotom-65 with cut-off wheel at a speed between 0.1 and 0.5 mm/s. From each bottle,
five samples are cut. The samples are placed in a climate-controlled room at 20 °C until the next day
when the samples are measured. The diameter is measured with a digital caliper at nine levels of the
sample height using stacked metal rings as seen in Figure 3.3. The lines on the sample indicate how the
sample should be placed on the metal plate such that the diameter is measured in the same direction
at every level. Each metal plate is 1.05 mm thick, but because of the bending of the plates, a total of
eight plates are stacked on top of each other to measure the top diameter of the samples. The metal
plate and rings are shown in Figure 3.3. Furthermore, the height of the sample is measured in four
places at each of the four drawn lines to get an average value, and the samples are also weighed.
Figure 3.3: Metal plate and rings used to measure the diameter.
Six samples are glued onto a plastic box. The plastic boxes have a volume of 1.2 L, with the dimensions
16.7 x 22.8 x 6.1 cm. Six holes of Ø38 mm are cut in the lid for the samples to lay on top of. A hole of
Ø12.5 mm is cut to fill up the container with the exposure solution. Silicone,Weicon Silicon-N neutral
vernetzend, is glued between the lid and container and between samples and the lid to minimize the
evaporation of the exposure solution. Approximately 2 to 3 hours later the box is filled with one of
the exposure solutions for the test, see Section 3.2, and placed in a climate chamber at 50 % relative
humidity and 40 °C.
The solution - when added - is at room temperature, and it is necessary to fill up with additional
solution three times a week, such that the samples remain in contact with the exposure solution. During
the test period, a small ball is put on top of the tube, from where the box is refilled, to slow down the
evaporation. The lid that is used to close off the box has a small curvature, such that after some of
the solution evaporates the samples in the middle of the box will lose contact first with the solution.
While the curvature of the box, is minimized to some extent by the weight from the samples, as the test
proceeds and samples are taken out for analysis, the weight on top of the lid becomes smaller. However,
samples are moved around every 4 weeks to mitigate the problem. Figure 3.4 shows a plastic box with
and without samples.
==PAGE:26==
14
3 Materials & Methodology
(a) Without samples.
(b) With samples.
Figure 3.4: Prepared plastic box.
Every 4 weeks, the samples are taken out of the climate chamber to be measured and weighed. They
are taken out in the late afternoon, cleaned for eventual crystals and the silicone is removed. Then
they are left in another climate chamber at 20 °C and 50 % relative humidity overnight to cool down.
When the samples have cooled down, they are weighed and the diameter is measured following the
same procedure as before the test starts. Next, the samples are glued back onto the lid. Furthermore,
the solution is changed with a new one. 20 mL samples are taken of the old solution for IC and ICP
analysis while crystals, that formed on the samples, are collected for XRD analysis. The test is run
for 12 weeks, with samples taken out for analysis at weeks 4, 8, and 12 after the beginning of the test.
However, samples exposed to 0.1 M of Na2SO4 are taken out for analysis in weeks 3, 5, and 7 after the
beginning of the test.
3.5.3
Naming of samples
Each box contains from the beginning of the test 6 samples numbered from 1-6. The samples are named
”A - B - C - D”, where A is the solution, B is the concentration, C is the sample number (from 1-6), and
D is the number of weeks the sample has been subjected to the exposure solution. If the sample has
been analyzed using X-ray diffraction (XRD), either the letter E, B, or T is added to indicate whether
the analysis is done on the edge, bottom, or top of the sample. Figure 3.5 shows the cross-section of a
sample and how the sample is divided.
B
T
E
E
Figure 3.5: Division of sample in top (T), bottom (B), and edge (E) for XRD analysis.
3.5.4
X-Ray diffraction
After being in the climate chamber for 4 weeks, one sample from each solution is taken out for analysis.
The sample is split into two pieces. Half of it is analyzed in the stereo microscope, Nikon SMZ25, and
the other half is analyzed using XRD. The sample is first crushed with a hammer and then ground
finely by hand in a mortar. The powder is pressed into the sample holder using a knife and a cylindrical
==PAGE:27==
3.5 Cement paste test
15
powder press block. Any excess powder is removed with a knife or a brush. The sample holder is filled
from the back to prevent a preferred orientation of the grains. The equipment used and a prepared
sample is shown in Figure 3.6. The cement paste used in this study has not been sieved in order to
control an adequate grain size. Instead, the material is ground by hand until the sample could be
pressed into the sample holder and remained intact when flipped.
The machine used for the XRD is called PANalytical X’Pert Pro and is set to Cu-Kα 2θ radiation
source with a voltage of 45 kV and current of 40 mA. A 1 ° divergence slit, a 10 mm mask spacing, and
a 6.6 ° anti-scatter slit are used. The samples are measured in the range of 5 ° - 70 ° for about 30 min
with an increasing step size 0.0170 ° 2θ, while the sample is spinning and the machine emits and receives
X-rays. To minimize the risk of carbonation during handling, the samples taken after either 3, 7, 8, or
12 weeks, are grounded and analyzed the same day. Samples taken after four weeks are analyzed in
the XRD a week after grinding but kept in a desiccator to minimize the risk of carbonation. Figure 3.6
shows the equipment used to prepare a sample and an empty and filled sample holder.
(a) Equipment for sample prepartion.
(b) empty sample holder (left) and filled sample holder (right).
Figure 3.6: Preparation of samples for XRD analysis.
Afterwards, the data is analyzed in the software Highscore by Malvern Panalytical by using the ICDD
database to identify phases based on the peaks.
3.5.5
Scanning Electron Microscope & energy-dispersive X-ray spectroscopy
SEM micrographs are obtained on cement pastes that have been exposed for 12 weeks in six of the seven
different solutions. The samples exposed to 0.1 M of Na2SO4 are only exposed for 7 weeks. The paste
samples are cut, stored in isopropanol alcohol for one day, dried at 40 °C, impregnated with low-viscosity
epoxy resin, polished, and carbon-coated (10 nm). The instrument used is an environmental scanning
electron microscopy FEI Quanta 200 (ESEM) equipped with a large field detector and an Oxford X
MaxN EDX detector that is operated with an acceleration voltage of 15 eV. SEM Micrographs of each
sample are obtained at the top, interface, and bottom, see Figure 3.7.

4.2
Leaching experiment
Leaching tests of the aggregate in the seven solutions are conducted by weighing the aggregate both
before and after the testing at either 40 degrees or 60 degrees, along with IC and ICP analysis of the
solution. Supplementary material can be found in Appendix C.
When the aggregate is mixed with 5 M H2O2 solution at 60 °C, a color change is observed, where
the solution goes from being clear to having a brown color. After being filtered, the solution is again
clear, but the aggregate has turned brown with some shiny particles. The other solutions do not change
color after 90 min but stay clear and the aggregate continues to have a grey color. Figure 4.2 shows the
color change after being tested at 60 °C in some of the solutions.
Reference
NaOCl - 1M
H2O2 - 1M
H2O2 - 5M
Figure 4.2: Color change of aggregate exposed to either NaOCl - 1M, H2O2 -1M or H2O2 -5M at 60
°C. To the left reference aggregate.
Table 4.1 shows either the increase or the decrease in mass after 90 min testing and drying at 105 °C.
When filtering the solution from the aggregate, there is a chance of losing a small amount of aggregate
in the process which can affect the weight measurement. Aggregates exposed to one of the chloride
solutions and the sulfate solution experienced a loss in mass at 40 °C, but a gain at 60 °C. Aggregate
exposed to the carbonate solution experienced an increase in weight at both temperatures, while the
hydrogen peroxide solutions both cause a loss in mass. However, the mass loss only increases when
going from 40 °C to 60 °C for the 5 M H2O2 and not for the 1 M H2O2. From this test, there does not
seem to be a correlation between the change in mass and the temperature change for every solution.

from the analysis can be found in Appendix C.2. From Figure 4.3 it is seen that the release of sulfur
and iron is largest for for solutions with 5 M of H2O2. There is also a release of sulfur into the solution
for the 1 M NaOCl solution, but there is not an equal release of iron into the solution.

4 Results
4.3
Oxygen measurement experiment
The oxygen concentration is measured with two different headspaces: 100 mm and 25 mm. With a
headspace of 100 mm, the oxygen meter becomes imprecise, when the maximum oxygen consumption
is reached of the aggregate.
With a cylinder diameter of 94 mm, the sensor gives the most stable
measurements with a headspace of 25 mm rather than 100 mm. Furthermore, two saturation degrees
are tested: 40 % and 50 %. The difference in saturation does not cause a great difference in oxygen
consumption, so a saturation degree of 50 % is opted for further analysis, as it corresponds the the
relative humidity used in the climate chamber with the cement paste samples.
Besides testing the
aggregate with deionized water, the different 1 M solutions are also tested along with 0.1 M Na2SO4.
The oxygen consumed in the different test setups is listed in Table 4.2. Note that in this case, only
the oxygen consumption is included. The complete data set from the measurements are summarised in
Appendix D.
Table 4.2: Oxygen consumption
Solution
Saturation degree [%]
Headspace [mm]
Oxygen consumption [%]
Deionized water
40
100
3.4
Deionized water
40
25
4.4
Deionized water
50
25
5.2
NaOCl - 1M
50
25
4.7
Na2CO3 - 1M
50
25
3.7
Na2SO4 - 0.1M
50
25
12.4
NaCl - 1M
50
25
14.4
As seen in Table 4.2, the aggregate mixed with deionized water consumes between 3-5 % oxygen.
Increasing the saturation degree to 50 %, leads to the highest level of oxygen consumption for aggregate
mixed with deionized water of 5.2 %. Aggregate mixed with either 1 M Na2CO3 or NaOCl consume
oxygen in the same range as when mixed with deionized water. However, when mixing the aggregate
with a solution of 1 M NaOCl, oxygen is released, and in the first 20 min of the test duration, the
oxygen concentration in the cylinder increases. Then after 30 min, the oxidation level starts to decrease.
Furthermore, condensation appears on the lid, indicating heat is created when mixing the aggregate
with the solution. The solution does not increase oxygen consumption, but it does speed up the process.
With deionized water, the oxygen concentration stabilizes after about two hours, while with 1 M NaOCl,
the oxygen concentration in the cylinder stabilizes one hour after the initial increase in oxygen. A test
is also set up with 1 M H2O2 as the solution but is stopped immediately because of a rapid increase
in the oxygen concentration within the cylinder. Aggregate mixed with 1 M NaCl and 0.1 M Na2SO4
show the highest amount of oxygen consumption with 14.4 % and 12.4 % respectively. Chlorides are
known to promote corrosion in reinforcement, as discussed in Section 2.4. Therefore, following the
same reasoning, it could be possible that chlorides enhance in this case the oxidation rate of pyrrhotite.
However, the oxygen consumption of aggregate mixed with 0.1 M Na2SO4 is nearly equally as high,
but does not contain chlorides or anything else known to promote oxidation of sulfide-bearing minerals.
Other reactions may take place at the same time which can cause the reduction in oxygen in the cylinder.
The test is based on previous studies, where only deionized water is used [4]. As for this study, there is
not a full understanding of all the reactions that might take place when mixing the different solutions
with the aggregate.

4.4
Cement paste experiment
4.4.1
Visual analysis
In the stereomicroscope, Nikon SMZ25, the cross section of the samples are examined. In general, for
every sample, a change in color on the air-side (edges and top), compared to the center and bottom is
observed as seen in Figure 4.4. The area where the color change occurs is named ”interface”. The reason
why the interface is not going all the way to the bottom is probably due to the silicone surrounding the
bottom of the sample.
10 mm
Figure 4.4: Picture of the cross-section of a sample taken in the stereomicroscope, Nikon SMZ25.
Some samples form crystals during exposure to the exposure solutions. When the samples are taken
out for expansion measurements, the crystals are removed, the sample is cleaned and the crystals are
sampled for XRD analysis. In Figure 4.5 the top of samples exposed to 1 M NaOCl and 0.1 M Na2SO4
are shown with the crystals that form after being in contact with the exposure solution.

The samples exposed to a sulfate solution form a smaller amount of crystals compared to samples
exposed to a chloride solution and the crystals are primarily composed of Na2SO4. Samples exposed to
a carbonate solution form white crystals on top, that can not be collected without breaking the sample,
and samples exposed to one of the two H2O2 solutions do not form any crystals. The samples exposed

solution, especially for samples exposed to 1 M NaOCl.
Two concentrations of sulfate are tested: 0.1 M and 1 M Na2SO4. Samples exposed to the solution
of 1 M broke after 2 weeks, which led to the termination of the test with that exposure solution. All
samples exposed to a 0.1 M Na2SO4 also broke within 7 weeks. Two samples broke after 3 weeks, while
the remaining broke after 7 weeks. However, the deterioration of the samples is less severe than of
the samples exposed to 1 M Na2SO4. A Sample exposed to 1 M Na2SO4 after 2 weeks is shown in

4.4.3
pH results
Figure 4.10 shows the change in pH over the 12 weeks of testing.
As there is a sample taken out
of the experiment every four weeks, the accumulated surface area of the samples that can react with
the solution decreases, while it is observed that the deterioration and leaching of the samples increases
which causes in turn the surface area of each sample, that remains in the experiment, to increase as well.
The change in pH in the initial solutions and the solution after exposure is greatest after 4 weeks, and
the difference decreases in weeks 8 and 12. The pH measurement can also be found in Appendix E.2.
The pH in the pore solution depends on the concentration of Na+, K+ and OH−ions. Most of the
exposure solutions have high concentrations of sodium, which can affect the pH in the pore solution.

4.4.4
IC/ICP results
As for the measurement of pH, the IC/ICP results show that the reaction between samples and exposure
solutions in general decreases with fewer samples in contact with the solution as the test proceeds.
However, it is still possible to see the leaching from the sample to the exposure solution by a change in
the concentration and the addition of other ions in the solution. Selected concentrations are shown in
the following, but all results from the IC/ICP results can be found in Appendix E.7. Please note that
in the the following graphs, the scale of the vertical axis differs between the graphs.
Figure 4.11 shows the leaching of calcium ions to the exposure solutions. It is shown that samples
exposed to a 1 M NaCl solution leach higher concentrations of calcium compared to the other tests.
Samples exposed to both concentrations of H2O2 also leach calcium, while samples exposed to 1 M
Na2CO3 or NaOCl solution show no or little leaching of calcium.

included in the C-S-H gel, which is the main binding phase in the cement paste. When silicium diffuses
out of the sample, it also indicates a loss of binding in the cement paste.

Potassium and sodium are two alkali metals, whose concentration influences the pH. A higher con-
centration of the metals will lead to a higher concentration of hydroxide and hence a higher pH [24].
==PAGE:38==
26
4 Results
Leaching of any alkali metal can then lead to a reduction of the pH in the pore solution. However,
nearly all exposure solutions, except the H2O2 solutions, contain sodium and have a pH above 7, so the
leaching of potassium will not likely decrease the pH due to high concentrations of sodium in the expo-
sure solutions. The leaching of potassium is shown in Figure 4.13, where it is seen that every sample
leaches some amount of potassium to the solution besides the sample exposed to a H2O2 solution. The
concentration is highest after 4 weeks and decreases in weeks 8 and 12.

The sulfur content in one cement paste sample of 30 g is estimated to be approximately 430 mg, where
120 mg is from the filler and 310 mg is from the cement. The estimate is based on the mix design of
the cement paste, Section 3.5.1, and an average sulfur content in the filler of 1.77 %, Section 3.1. The
Aalborg Rapid® cement used contains less than 4 % of SO3, which leads to an estimate of 1.6 % of
sulfur in the cement [22, 23]. The calculations can be found in Appendix E.1. Six samples contain
then approximately 2600 mg of sulfur, which potentially can be released into the solution. As seen
in Figure 4.15, the amount of sulfur released in the solution happens throughout the 12 weeks, and
as the samples are removed continuously the total amount of sulfur in the solution cannot reach the
amount of sulfur within six samples. However, it gives an idea of how much sulfur that can diffuse out
of the samples and how much that actually has diffused out of the samples. The total amount of sulfur
concentration in the exposure solution was highest for Na2SO4, but this is also the only solution that
contains sulfur from the beginning. The sulfur concentration of Na2CO3 is significantly higher than the
rest, followed by the NaOCl solution. The total amount of S in the solution after 12 weeks of testing
in Na2CO3 is similar to the amount of S found in one sample. From the ICP analysis, it is not possible
to say how much of the sulfur comes from the cement and how much comes from the filler.
Figure 4.17 shows the leaching of iron into the solution. The amount of iron released into the solution
is very low compared to the leaching of the other ions mentioned. Still, it is included as the amount
of soluble iron in the solution can indicate that an oxidation of pyrrhotite has occurred. 1 M Na2CO3
shows the largest amount of iron ions in the solution. The two solutions with NaOCl show an increase
after 8 weeks, while 5 M H2O2 shows an increase after 12 weeks.

In general, the IC/ICP results show an exchange of ions between the samples and solutions, mainly
where ions are leaching out of the samples and into the solution. However, from the visual analysis,
sodium, chloride, and sulfate form crystals on top of the samples, so there is also an exchange the
other way from the solution to the sample. The solution of 1 M NaCl shows increased concentration
of both calcium, potassium, and sulfur after being in contact with the samples.
The solutions of
NaOCl show increased concentrations of both potassium and sulfur after being in contact samples. The
solution of 1M of Na2CO3 shows increased concentrations of potassium, silicium, sulfur, and iron after
being in contact with the samples. The solution of 0.1M of Na2SO4 shows increased concentrations of
calcium and potassium after being in contact with the samples. The samples exposed to either of the
H2O2 solutions hardly show any exchange between the sample and solution. However, there are found
precipitates after 8 weeks and 12 weeks, so leaching from the samples occurs, but it is not dissolved in
==PAGE:41==

the solution. The exposure solution of Na2CO3 shows the greatest increased concentration of most of
the ions.
4.4.5
Horizontal expansion of samples
The diameter of the sample is measured at nine different levels along the height every 4 weeks to
measure a potential horizontal expansion due to sulfate attack.
The height is measured as well to
identify a potential vertical expansion in the sample, but in this case, there is no correlation identified
between samples from the same exposure solution. The measurements of the diameter can be found in
Appendix E.5, while the measurement of the heights can be found in Appendix E.4. Figure 4.18 and
Figure 4.19 show the average expansion in % of the samples exposed to their respective solution, where
height = 0 mm is the bottom of the sample and height = 100 mm is the top of the sample. In the
bottom left corner of the graph, the standard deviation of the expansion between the samples in their
respective solutions is depicted.
Expansion measurement is a simple quantitative method to follow the deterioration process due to
a sulfate attack. The expansion measurement by a caliper and metal plates is not the most precise
method. However, it is possible to identify a general trend of the expansion. The samples do not
expand the same every month. Therefore, a standard deviation for every solution is indicated in the
figure, and for every solution, the average standard deviation is between 0.10 - 0.16 %, except for 1 M
H2O2 where the standard deviation is 0.23 %
Figure 4.18 shows the expansion of samples exposed to 0.1 M Na2SO4 under an external sulfate
attack. The sample is expanding the most at the bottom of the sample. The average expansion shown
in Figure 4.18 only includes samples 1, 2, 3, and 4, because while samples 5 and 6 show great expansion
after 3 weeks, they crack after 5 weeks. Sample number 3, was taken out for analysis after 3 weeks,
while samples 1, 2, 3, and 4 show small cracks in week 7. Therefore the expansion measurements of 4
and 6 are not included along with expansion measurements from week 7.

Figure 4.18: Expansion of samples exposed to 0.1 M Na2SO4.
Figure 4.19 shows the expansion measurements of samples exposed to the remaining solution, where
the expansion is not expected to be due to an external sulfate attack, but rather an internal sulfate
attack, where the sulfur is released from the oxidation of pyrrhotite.
==PAGE:42==
30
4 Results
Similar to the expansion of Na2SO4 samples, the NaOCl samples experience a larger expansion at the
bottom than at the top, but samples exposed to 1 M NaCl solution, show no clear trend of expansion in
the sample. Samples exposed to one of the three chloride solutions form crystals during testing. These
crystals are removed before measuring the diameter of the sample. However, some crystals can not be
removed without damaging the sample. This will inevitably add up in the measurement of the diameter
and therefore increase the expansion. However, by measuring the diameter in four different places at
every level and cleaning the samples, the effect of the leftover crystals is minimized.
The samples exposed to 1 M Na2CO3 solution show shrinkage after 4 weeks, and expansion after
8 weeks. The expansion after 8 weeks does not show the same trend as the samples exposed to 0.1 M
Na2SO4 or the NaOCl solutions with only expansion in the bottom. Instead, the expansion is larger
both at the top and bottom of the samples. The Na2CO3 samples crack before 12 weeks and show
spalling on the surface, so there are no measurements of the expansion at week 12.
The samples exposed to either 1 M or 5 M H2O2 solutions do not show a great change in the diameter
and the average expansions of samples exposed to either 1 M or 5 M H2O2 do not change significantly
between week 4 and 12.
All samples, except the samples exposed to 1 M NaOCl, experience shrinkage after the first 4 weeks
of exposure, which can be due to the low relative humidity in the climate chamber [8].

4 Results
4.4.6
XRD results
Samples from the top, bottom, and edge parts of the cement paste samples are collected every 4 weeks
for XRD analysis. The visual investigation of the samples shows that there is a clear color difference
between the bottom, top, and edge of the sample, as discussed in Section 4.4.1. The XRD is used as a
qualitative assessment of the composition of the cement paste.
The main constituents in cement are portlandite (CH), and calcite (Cc). These peaks are identified
first. Of the AFm phases, monocarbonate (Mc), hemicarbonate (Hc), and Friedel’s salt (FS) are identi-
fied. Friedel’s salt and NaCl are both found in samples exposed to a chloride solution as expected and
ettringite (Ett) is also identified. Thernadite (Th) peaks are identified in crystals formed on samples
exposed to 0.1 M Na2SO4, but not in the sample. From the aggregate, biotite (B), plagioclase (P), and
quartz (Q) are identified. The reference codes for the different peaks can be found in Appendix E.8. It
is not possible to identify pyrrhotite or pyrite in the cement paste samples by the XRD, as the amount
is too small and their peaks are diluted by the cement and the other minerals in the filler material.
XRD-analysis is also carried out on reference samples, that have been left in a closed plastic bottle
with damp paper in a climate-controlled room at 20 °C. XRD analysis is done on the sample continuously
to compare with the samples in the climate chamber. Therefore, reference samples of respectively 2,
2+3, 2+8, and 2+12 weeks of curing are analyzed.
Figure 4.20, Figure 4.21 and Figure 4.22 show the XRD results from the bottom of the samples at
4 weeks, 8 weeks, and 12 weeks respectively.

==PAGE:46==
34
4 Results
From the filler, peaks of biotite, plagioclase, and quartz are identified. Peaks of hemicarbonate and
monocarbonate are found in the reference samples. Of the exposed samples, it is mainly the samples
exposed to H2O2 that show peaks of monocarbonate. Instead, Friedel’s salt is found in samples exposed
to one of the chloride solutions, and ettringite is found in samples exposed to 0.1 M Na2SO4. The
crystals on the surface of the sample consist mainly of NaCl and NaCl was also identified as a peak in
the cement paste, while thernadite was not identified in samples exposed to 0.1 M Na2SO4 even though
crystals formed on top.
The peak of portlandite in samples exposed to the different solutions varies within the same frame
of exposure and is smaller compared to the reference sample. Little or no portlandite is left in the
bottom of the samples exposed 1 M Na2CO3 already after 4 weeks, and it is seen that portlandite is
affected by the carbonation and the formation of calcite. The peak of portlandite is most predominant
in samples exposed to one of the H2O2 solutions in the bottom of the samples compared to the other
solutions. Saturated areas are not prone to carbonation to the same extent as dry areas as described in
Section 2.5. Carbonation occurs when there is a source of water and carbon dioxide, which is typically
the air but can also be from a solution such as Na2CO3. The parts of the sample in contact with the
air, are the top and edge, but for the samples exposed to Na2CO3, the bottom is also in contact with
a source of carbon dioxide. Every 4 weeks, the samples are taken out of the climate chamber, and for
approximately 24 hours the samples are not in contact with a solution but kept in a chamber at 50 %
relative humidity. When handling the samples, cleaning, measuring, etc., the bottom of the samples
is not in contact with a solution, and the possibility of carbonation increases with the drying of the
sample.
Figure 4.23 shows the peak of calcite in samples exposed to Na2CO3 in the bottom, edge, and top
after 8 weeks along with sample exposed to 1 M H2O2 and 1 M NaOCl. For the samples exposed
to Na2CO3 the peak of calcite is higher at the bottom and edge compared to the top, while for the
remaining exposure solutions, the peak for calcite is higher for both the edge and top. As the Na2CO3
contains carbonate ions, it contributes to the formation of calcite. For samples exposed to the other
solutions, the carbonation occurs primarily at the air side of the samples, while the bottom does not
experience carbonation to the same extent as the top and bottom. Figures for the remaining exposure
solutions can be found in Appendix E.9.

A clear peak for ettringite appears only in the samples exposed to 0.1 M Na2SO4. Small humps are
however still visible for the other samples, except for 1 M Na2CO3, indicating that some poor crystalline
ettringite is present. Figure 4.24 shows the peak of ettringite in 0.1 M Na2SO4, where (a) shows the
increase in ettringite in weeks 3 and 7, and (b) shows that the formation of ettringite mainly occurs in
the bottom of the sample. The oxidation of pyrrhotite releases sulfur, which in reaction with portlandite
can form gypsum that again will react with monosulfate and form ettringite, see Section 2.2. However,
the ettringite in samples exposed to 0.1 M Na2SO4, is more likely due to the sulfur concentration in the
solution than the oxidation of pyrrhotite. Even though ettringite is present in most samples, it is also
present in the reference sample, so it is diﬀicult to say if the solution has caused oxidation of pyrrhotite
and sulfur release.

Figure 4.25: Peaks of Friedel’s salt in the bottom of samples for weeks 4, 8, and 12.
While the peaks of Friedel’s salt appear at the same angle for samples exposed to NaCl as the test
proceeds, the angle where the peaks of Friedel’s salt occur for samples exposed to NaOCl increases in
function of the elapsed time of the test. Friedel’s salt is dependent on the pH in the pore solution and
studies have been previously done on adding HCl to samples exposed to NaCl, showing that the angle
of the peak of Friedel’s salt increases with increasing acid volumes and hence a decrease in the pH.
They found that lowering the pH of the pore solution from 13 to 12 increased the chloride binding, but
lowering the pH below 12 would lead to a reduced chloride binding in part due to a dissolution of AFm
[18].

4.4.7
SEM & EDX results
After 12 weeks, one sample from each solution is selected for SEM and EDX. Furthermore, atoms are
mapped based on the obtained micrographs to facilitate the identification of the present constituents in
the area. In Appendix E.10, relevant micrographs, spectra, and mapping of certain ions are included.
As there are no micrographs of a reference sample, a micrograph of a common cement paste is used
as a reference to compare the microstructure to, see Figure 4.26.
Hence, in case a loose or dense
microstructure is mentioned, this is always relative to the reference micrograph.
Micrographs are
taken of the top of the sample, of the interface, and of the bottom of the sample, see Figure 4.27 and
Figure 4.28. This is a general investigation and the samples should be more thoroughly analyzed to get
a full understanding of the transport and degradation mechanisms in the samples.

Figure 4.28: Micrographs from the top (T), interface (I), and bottom (B) of samples belonging to
each exposure solution after 12 weeks of exposure, except it is 7 weeks for sample exposed to 0.1 M
Na2SO4. The 7-week sample of Na2SO4 cracked in the interface and IT indicates that the micrograph
is from the top of the interface, above the crack.
In general, the top surface shows a loose microstructure with a densified area in the interface, and
leaching from the bottom surface of the sample. A picture of the whole interface is depicted in Fig-
ure 3.7. The interface is the drying front of the sample, where the sample has dried on the air side,
but is still saturated in the ”center” of the sample causing the ions to accumulate. While most samples
show visible signs of an interface, the location of the interface differs. When the interface is mentioned,
it does not refer to a certain distance from the surface of the sample, but an area at the top where
there is a clear change in microstructure, eg. densified microstructure or a crack. There seems to be a
tendency for horizontal cracks in the interface or vertical cracks in the bottom of the samples.
From the micrographs of the samples exposed to one of the NaOCl solutions, Figure 4.27 (a) - (f), the
interface is less clear than the ones seen in other samples. On the top, the microstructure is in general
loose but becomes denser underneath the interface. There are several examples of what is assumed to
be oxidized pyrrhotite, where a mineral of iron and sulfur contains veins of oxidation products, but
there are no clear signs of the formation of ettringite observed. Especially the sample exposed to 1 M
NaOCl, shows a concentration of vertical cracks in the bottom. Sodium chloride is especially present
==PAGE:52==
40
4 Results
above the interface as seen in the interface of 0.4 M NaOCl, Figure 4.27 (b). Also in the sample exposed
to 1 M NaCl, there seems to be a layer on top of the cross section in the top and interface part of the
sample, Figure 4.27 (g) and (h), which might be due to bad polishing.
The sample exposed to 1 M NaCl shows a loose microstructure and cracks throughout the sample,
Figure 4.27 (g) - (i). The many cracks in the sample can be due to the salt crystallization of NaCl
causing expansion of the sample. The top of the sample is primarily of sodium chloride. At the bottom
surface in contact with the solution, there is a densified layer of primarily accumulated calcium.
Micrographs of the sample exposed to Na2CO3, Figure 4.27 (j)-(l), show a loose microstructure
throughout the sample. In the interface, the structure is a little denser, and cracks are going horizontally
in the interface. On the bottom surface, there is a layer rich in calcium, aluminum, and sodium.
The sample exposed to 0.1 M Na2SO4 after 7 weeks, Figure 4.28 (a)-(c), shows a loose microstructure
in the top and a crack going horizontally in the interface. The microstructure around this crack is
denser than the microstructure above. Figure 4.28 (b) shows the microstructure above the crack, and
the microstructure of the area below does not show a densification to the same extent as above. Above
the crack are higher amounts of sulfur present. While there is a clear change in the microstructure
at the top of the sample, the change is less clear below the crack. At the bottom surface in contact
with the solution, there is leaching of accumulated calcium. The crack in the interface can be due to
a physical sulfate attack, with the crystallization of Na2SO4 or accumulation of ions, see Section 2.3.
There is no sign of oxidation of pyrrhotite.
Samples exposed to both of H2O2 solutions, Figure 4.28 (d)-(i), have a loose microstructure through-
out the sample, whereas in the other samples, the loose microstructure is mainly limited to above the
interface. However, there is a visible interface where the microstructure is denser with cracks going
horizontally. The sulfur content decreases significantly above the interface. Iron-sulfur minerals are
detected, but there is no sign of iron oxides, that can indicate the mineral has oxidized. At the bottom
surface in contact with the solution, there is a densified layer of primarily accumulated calcium. An
analysis of the solid precipitates observed in the bottom of the 5 M H2O2 after 8 and 12 weeks shows
a mix of iron oxides, aggregates, and cementitious products.
Figure 4.29 shows two particles of iron and sulfur in samples exposed to 0.4 M NaOCl and 1 M
NaOCl respectively. Both particles show signs of oxidation. The white part is of iron and sulfur, while
the slightly more grey parts are of iron and oxygen. The oxidation pattern of the particle is similar to
oxidized pyrrhotite particles found in the houses in Donegal, Ireland [5].

CHAPTER 5
Discussion
5.1
Solutions
A summary of the results related to the detection of oxidation of pyrrhotite or sulfate attack of each
testing method for each solution is shown in Table 5.1. Each solution is discussed thoroughly regarding
the outcomes of the different test methods.
Based on a previous study done by Rodrigues et al., where a solution of 1 M NaOCl proved successful
in oxidizing pyrrhotite, the same concentration is used for every solution in this study. However, it might
have created harmful environments for the samples, causing the destabilization of the hydration products
rather than promoting the oxidation of pyrrhotite. In the cement paste test, a relative humidity of 50
% and temperature of 40 °C are chosen to enhance the oxidation of pyrrhotite. However, carbonation
is also enhanced in relative humidities between 50 % and 70 %, see Section 2.5.
5.1.1
Chloride solutions
Based on the ICP results of the leaching tests, the solutions of 1 M NaOCl and 0.4 M NaOCl show
minimal amounts of iron in solutions after testing (between 0.01 and 0.09 mg/L), while NaCl did not
show any leaching of iron into the solution, see Figure 4.3. There is therefore no clear sign of oxidation
following the leaching test with any of the chloride solutions.
In the oxidation measurement test, the oxidation consumption using NaCl reaches the highest per-
centage of the solutions with 14.4 %. Chlorides are known to accelerate the oxidation of metal, see
Section 2.4, causing pitting corrosion and forming iron oxides. Similar mechanisms can have occurred
here. NaOCl is known to be an oxidizing agent, but the oxygen used, that causes the oxidation, origi-
nates rather from the solution than from the air. Hence it makes it impossible to measure the oxygen
consumption with an oxygen meter, where the oxygen concentration increases within the cylinder, while
there might be oxidation processes occurring simultaneously.
The amount of crystals formed on the cement paste samples is the largest on samples exposed to 1
M NaOCl, which is also the solution with the highest concentration of chloride ions, see Table 4.3 and
Figure 4.14. From Figure 4.18, the expansion of samples exposed to 1 M NaOCl and 0.4 M NaOCl show
greater expansion in the bottom similar to the trend of 0.1 M Na2SO4. While the samples exposed
to 1 M NaOCl solution show greater expansion than the ones exposed to 0.4 M NaOCl, the XRD
results did not show any clear peaks indicating ettringite is formed in the bottom of the samples, see
Section 4.4.6. Instead, Friedel’s salt is formed indicating the binding of the chloride ions. However, the
angle of the peak’s occurrence increases, which can be linked to small decreases in pH. Below a pH of
12, the chloride binding decreases partly due to the dissolution of the AFm phases and approaches 0
at a pH of 9 [18]. The shifting of Friedel’s salt phases can then be linked to decreases in the pH of the
pore solution, where the increase in angle is linked to the decrease in pH. From the SEM analysis, see
Section 4.4.7, a strong pattern of vertical cracks, concentrated in the bottom of the sample exposed to

5.1 Solutions
