1 M NaOCl in 12 weeks, can be linked to the horizontal expansion also measured in the bottom of the
sample, but not directly to a chemical sulfate attack as no evidence of ettringite is found either in the
XRD or SEM analysis. However, oxidation products are identified in the 12-week samples in SEM, and
small increases of sulfur and iron are measured in the exposure solution via ICP. The brown-colored
pop-outs found on the bottom of the sample might be iron oxide from the oxidation. A more thorough
study in the microscope can maybe clarify the cause of the cracks and if it is from a chemical sulfate
attack or maybe salt crystallization of NaCl.
The samples exposed to NaCl show shrinkage after the first 4 weeks and continue to show shrinkage
in the top also 8 weeks after.
However, the bottom of the samples starts to show little expansion
compared to the first 4 weeks. From the SEM-analysis, the sample exposed to 1 M NaCl for 12 weeks,
shows great amounts of vertical cracks throughout the sample, which can have led to the horizontal
expansion of the samples. Iron-sulfur minerals are found in the sample but no iron oxides indicating
that the mineral has oxidized. However, the formation of the cracks might also be due to the salt
crystallization of NaCl. Similar to the samples exposed to NaOCl, the samples exposed to NaCl did
not show greater peaks of ettringite, than the ones in the reference sample. The peaks of ettringite are
formed, but whether it is due to the hydration of the cement or it is due to the oxidation of pyrrhotite
is not clear.
5.1.2
Sulfate solution
In the leaching test using 0.1 M Na2SO4 as the exposure solution, no indication of the oxidation of
pyrrhotite has been found as there is no significant increase in the concentration of sulfur nor iron in
the exposure solution.
In the oxygen measurement test mixing the aggregate with the 0.1 M Na2SO4 solution results in an
oxygen consumption of 12.4 %. As this solution has not previously been proven to oxidize iron, it is
assumed that the decrease in oxygen within the cylinder is due to other reactions between the solution
and aggregate. As the mix is not analyzed afterward, the reactions occurring are unknown.
The test setup with cement paste sample exposed to 0.1 M Na2SO4 only went on for 7 weeks, as
originally the exposure solution of 1 M was used, but caused a severe sulfate attack in the samples after
2 weeks. However, for both solutions, cracks formed in the interface - both on the top and also the edge.
This can be due to the physical sulfate attack, where alkali sulfates crystallize just below the surface.
The ions move in the pore solutions by capillary action and diffusion but stop at the drying front because
the water has evaporated from the surface. This leads to salt crystallization of the alkali sulfates and
expansion, see Section 2.3, [8]. In the micrographs of the interface in the sample exposed for 7 weeks,
the microstructure is densified with significantly larger concentrations of sulfur and sodium supporting
the theory above that the movement of sodium and sulfur stopped due to the water evaporation on
the surface. Peaks of ettringite are found in the XRD-analysis, that are greater than the ones found
in the reference or the other samples. The ettringite is concentrated in the bottom, but peaks are also
found on the top, and edge of the sample, see Figure 4.24. Compared with expansion measured in the
samples (mainly in the bottom, see Figure 4.18), the conclusion is that it is due to a chemical sulfate
attack. There is an increase in the concentration of sulfur in the exposure, solution, but it is likely, not
due to the oxidation of pyrrhotite, but rather due to the continuously adding of new solution to keep
the sample in contact with the exposure solution.
==PAGE:58==
46
5 Discussion
5.1.3
Carbonate solution
In the leaching test using 1 M Na2CO3 as the exposure solution, no indication for the oxidation of
pyrrhotite has been found as there is no significant increase in the concentration of sulfur nor iron in
the exposure solution.
Also in the oxygen measurement test mixing the aggregate with the 1 M Na2CO3 solution resulted
in an oxygen consumption of 3.7 %, which is not significantly different from when using deionized water,
hence the solution does not promote oxidation according to this test setup.
The cement paste samples exposed to 1 M Na2CO3 solution show spalling on the surface and all the
remaining samples have cracked before 12 weeks. The XRD-analysis shows that most of the portlandite
forms calcite carbonate within the first 4 weeks of exposure, and the samples mainly consist of calcite
by the end of the test, see Figure 4.20. The samples also experience the highest increase in density,
which can be due to the formation of calcite and a decrease in porosity. While samples exposed to other
solutions show greater formation of calcite on the edge and top of the sample, the samples exposed to
Na2CO3 also form calcite in the bottom due to the carbonate solution, see Figure 4.23. As the samples
have completely deteriorated, it explains why they seem to leach more than the other solutions with
the leaching of potassium, silicium, sulfur, and iron. The SEM-analysis shows a loose microstructure,
but no sign of oxidized pyrrhotite. In neither of the three tests, the solution shows signs of promoting
the oxidation of pyrrhotite.
5.1.4
Hydrogenperoxide solutions
Based on the ICP results of the leaching tests, the solution of 5 M H2O2 causes the oxidation of the
pyrrhotite with increased concentrations of iron and sulfur in the exposure solution, see Figure 4.3. The
concentrations of iron and sulfur increase with an increase in the temperature from 40 °C to 60 °C.
Using 1 M H2O2 as exposure solution also causes oxidation, but is less prominent. For a short test to
examine the oxidation of pyrrhotite, a solution of 5 M H2O2 is preferred.
In the oxygen measurement test, a mix of aggregate and H2O2 is not optimal, as the mix will
significantly increase the oxygen level within the cylinder, so it is not possible to measure any oxygen
consumption.
Both H2O2 solutions have initially a low pH, that increases when in contact with the cement paste
samples, see Figure 4.10. The lower the pH of the acid, the worse the chemical attack on the cement
paste [26].
The acid environment causes breakage of the C-S-H gel and release of calcium ions as
also seen Figure 4.11. Having the cement paste samples in a highly acid solution essentially causes an
acid attack on the samples, which can manifest detrimental physical effects such as spalling as seen
in Figure 4.8 (b) or an increase in porosity, which is seen by the decrease in density of the samples
in Figure 4.9. Samples exposed to either of the H2O2 solutions show a decrease in density, while only
samples exposed to 5 M H2O2 show visible spalling of the cement paste after 12 weeks. From the XRD
analysis, the samples show peaks of both monocarbonate and ettringite, Figures 4.20 to 4.22, indicating
that the AFm phases have not completely destabilized due to the decrease in pH. Based on the IC and
ICP analysis, there is no significant leaching of any compound. The solid precipitates found in the
bottom of the 5 M H2O2 solution after 8 and 12 weeks consist of a mix of iron, filler material, and
hydration products which can be linked to the spalling of the bottom of the samples, see Appendix E.10,
Figure 4.8 and Section 4.4.7. Samples exposed to either H2O2 solutions, show a loose microstructure in
the SEM analysis with a densified layer in the interface, but no sign of pyrrhotite oxidation. However,
the acid environment created around the samples is eventually a bigger problem for the cement paste
than pyrrhotite oxidation [10].
==PAGE:59==
CHAPTER 6
Conclusion
The objective of the study, is to investigate the oxidation and sulfur release of a sulfide-bearing aggregate
with a high content of especially pyrrhotite and how to best elevate the oxidation of the aggregate by
itself and when mixed in concrete. Based on three different test methods the problems are assessed,
and it can be concluded that all three test methods, with the right solution, can assess the oxidation
potential of a sulfide-bearing aggregate. However, it can also be concluded that one solution is not the
most optimal for every test set up, so depending on the test different solutions should be used.
When testing only the aggregate in a leaching test, a solution of 5 M H2O2 at 60 °C is proven most
successful in elevating the oxidation and sulfur release of the aggregate, while the other solutions do
not cause any measurable release of sulfur or iron.
The other test, which only tests the aggregate, hence the oxidation measurement test, 1 M NaCl
mixed with the aggregate reached an oxygen consumption of 14.4 %, which can be due to reactions
similar to pitting corrosion of steel reinforcement. However, in this test setup, it turned out to be
diﬀicult to test the oxygen consumption of the different mixes, as the oxygen meter only measures the
oxygen consumed in the air and not from the solution.
When testing the aggregate in a cement paste, the exposure solutions result in different outcomes
in the samples. The test investigates the oxidation of pyrrhotite and the degradation mechanisms due
to a potential internal sulfate attack. However, degradation of the sample can also be due to some of
the solutions, as they can create a harmful environment for the samples causing the destabilization of
ettringite and the AFm phases, such as a delayed ettringite formation from the oxidation of pyrrhotite
is impossible.
Only samples exposed to 0.1 M Na2SO4 show clear peaks of ettringite in the XRD
analysis, indicating the occurrence of a sulfate attack. However, this is not due to the sulfur release
from pyrrhotite, but a result of the sulfates in the exposure solution. In a sample exposed to 1 M NaOCl
for 12 weeks, oxidation products are found, along with vertical cracks concentrated and expansion in
the bottom. Even though there is no clear sign of delayed ettringite formation in these cracks, oxidized
pyrrhotite is found in the samples. The solution shows the most promising results in accelerating the
oxidation of pyrrhotite and the release of sulfur. As one of the objectives of the study is to investigate a
sulfate attack caused by the oxidation of pyrrhotite, it can be concluded, that there is no clear evidence
that the oxidation initiated a sulfate attack in the cement paste samples. However, the samples must
be studied further.
It can therefore be concluded that depending on whether only the aggregate tested or the aggregate
used in a cement paste or concrete, is tested, different solutions are appropriate. While a solution
of H2O2 might cause a release of sulfur, the solution is not applicable in a cementitious environment,
because of its low pH, which causes a destabilization of the hydration products. Here a solution with
high pH, like NaOCl, is preferred to keep the hydration products stable during the test.
==PAGE:60==
48
==PAGE:61==
CHAPTER 7
Further work
Based on this study and the three different testing methods, some new aspects could be investigated
further to close the existing knowledge gaps.
Both the oxygen measurement test and the cement paste test showed signs of oxidation, with the
use of solutions containing chloride ions. Since chlorides are known to promote the oxidation of steel
rebars in concrete, it would therefore be interesting to investigate further how the chlorides affect the
oxidation of pyrrhotite.
The different solutions not only affect the oxidation process but can also cause other reactions in
the aggregate and the cement paste. From the oxygen measurement test, oxygen was consumed in the
cylinder. However, it is not clear whether the decrease in oxygen is due to oxidation of pyrrhotite or
other reactions occurring between the aggregate and the different solutions. An analysis of the mix
after testing could lead to a better understanding of the reactions occurring in the aggregate during
testing.
In the cement paste test, intending to elevate the oxidation of pyrrhotite, some of the solutions
might have affected the pH of the pore solution leading to a destabilization of the hydration products.
For further studies in the oxidation of pyrrhotite, pH correction of the solutions should be done such
that the AFm and AFt phases are not destabilized and the formation of eg. ettringite is possible. The
samples analyzed using SEM are studied in three points of the sample. A further investigation of the
samples can lead to a better understanding of the transport and degradation mechanisms in the sample
and the densification of microstructure in the interface.
